         <div id="print_ep" class="print_ep">
          <a id="EP" name="EP"></a> 
          <p id="printIcon" class="printIcon" align="right"><a onclick="printContent('print_ep')" href="#EP"><img src="/images/printer.gif" alt="Print" width="24" height="24" align="top" border="0" hspace="4">Print</a></p> 
          <h3>Extraction Products FAQs</h3> 
          <p>TTB has discovered many herb, flower, gem and mineral extraction products being labeled, marketed and sold at retail in the United States as dietary supplements or "natural health remedies." We have determined that many of these products are fit for beverage purposes and contain 0.5 percent or more alcohol by volume (ABV), making them subject to regulation and taxation as alcohol beverages. These Frequently Asked Questions (FAQs) are intended to provide general guidance to the public and to producers, distributors, and importers of extraction products. If you have any questions about this guidance, please contact TTB's Alcohol Labeling, and Formulation Division at 2024532250 or Toll Free at 866927ALFD (2533) or at submit an <a href="/contact-alfd">online inquiry</a>.</p> 
          <h3>General</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu1" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep1" name="ep1"></a>EP1: What are extraction products?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub1" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>While there is some variation in production methods, extraction products are generally produced by steeping herbs, flowers, gems or minerals in water, which is used as an extracting agent. The steeping process is believed to extract various elements from the steeped material which are beneficial to the user of the extraction product. The herbs, flowers, gems or minerals are removed after steeping, and alcohol, usually vodka or brandy, is added to the water as a preservative. In some cases alcohol may be used as the extracting agent. Extraction products are generally sold as sprays or drops, usually in one or two ounce containers. These products are generally marketed, advertised and labeled as "essences," but they are not the same as fruit or other essences that are commercially produced and intended for use as flavorings for alcohol beverages or other foods.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="EPmenu2" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep2" name="ep2"></a>EP2: Are extraction products alcohol beverages?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub2" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB's initial testing of extraction products in the marketplace revealed that all of the products tested had 0.5 percent or more alcohol by volume (ABV) which is the threshold alcohol content for products to be regulated as alcohol beverages, unless they are unfit for beverage purposes (See FAQ EP12). The extraction products tested ranged in alcohol content from 3.2 percent to 27.9 percent ABV. Testing by TTB revealed that many of the extraction products were fit for beverage purposes and subject to taxation and regulation, while some were found to be unfit for beverage purposes.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="EPmenu3" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep3" name="ep3"></a>EP3: What are TTB's recent actions regarding extraction products in the marketplace?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub3" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>We have analyzed samples of extraction products in the marketplace, and tested the samples for alcohol content and fitness for beverage purposes. We will work with producers, distributors, or importers of extraction products so that they may comply with law and regulations and ensure that their products are appropriately labeled. Our primary concern is that consumers be adequately informed about the nature of alcohol beverage products being be marketed as something else, such as dietary supplements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Labeling of Extraction Products</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu4" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep4" name="ep4"></a>EP4: Does the Federal Alcohol Administration Act (FAA Act) apply to extraction products?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub4" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you are a producer, importer, or wholesaler of extraction products that are alcohol beverages (<em>i.e.</em> 0.5 percent or more ABV and fit for beverage purposes) you may be subject to the permit, labeling, and advertising requirements of the FAA Act, depending on the classification of the alcohol beverage product.* The determination of whether an extraction product, that is an alcohol beverage, falls within the classification of a wine, malt beverage, or distilled spirits product under the FAA Act must be made on a case-by-case basis, depending on the formulation and method of production.</p> 
            <p>Some alcohol beverages are subject to tax as wine or beer, but are not subject to the requirements of the FAA Act. For example, wine containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, and beer that is not made with both malted barley and hops, are exempt from the labeling requirements under the FAA Act and must comply with FDA labeling requirements, as well as the marking requirements of the Internal Revenue Code of 1986 (IRC), as amended.</p> 
            <p>* For more information on the FAA Act, see <a href="http://frwebgate.access.gpo.gov/cgi-bin/usc.cgi?ACTION=BROWSE&amp;TITLE=27USCC8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 U.S.C. 201</a>, et seq.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="EPmenu5" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep5" name="ep5"></a>EP5: Are the containers of extraction products required to bear a health warning statement?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub5" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The container of any alcohol beverage product sold or distributed in the United States with an alcohol content of 0.5 percent ABV or more must bear the health warning statement required by the Alcoholic Beverage Labeling Act of 1988 (ABLA).* This requirement applies regardless of whether the product is subject to the labeling requirements of the FAA Act.</p> 
            <p>* For more information on ABLA and the TTB regulation, see <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2011-title27/html/USCODE-2011-title27-chap8-subchapII-sec215.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 U.S.C. 215</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi/t/text/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=fd8b0ad16b93584273aefb7460a98eb4&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr16_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="EPmenu6" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep6" name="ep6"></a>EP6: What rules apply to health claims on the labels or in advertisements for extraction products?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub6" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Due to the fact that extraction products are generally sold as dietary supplements or "natural health remedies," TTB has observed that the labels and advertising for the products often contain claims regarding the health effects derived from using the products.</p> 
            <p>If the products are determined to be alcohol beverages (<em>i.e.</em> 0.5 percent ABV and fit for beverage purposes) any claims on labels or in advertising for the products would be subject to the labeling and advertising requirements of the FAA Act and the TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=506cf0c03546efff958847134c5527d3&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a>, <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=0b1336cf26cbf0cd359ebd43d02b8706&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.3&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 5</a> or <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=d473aa9e89b3f7135e18c8da635f26aa&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr7_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 7</a>. This includes the regulations regarding health-related statements.</p> 
            <p>Health-related statements are generally defined as statements related to health, including statements of a curative or therapeutic nature that, expressly or by implication, suggest a relationship between the consumption of alcohol beverages, or any substance found within alcohol beverages, and health benefits or effects on health. Health-related statements include both specific health claims and general references to alleged health benefits, or effects on health as well as health-related directional statements such as those terms which are defined in the regulations. Health-related statements also include statements and claims that imply that a physical or psychological sensation results from consuming the alcohol, as well as statements and claims of nutritional value (<em>e.g., </em>statements of vitamin content).</p> 
            <p>In general, advertisements may not contain any health-related statement that is untrue in any particular, or tends to create a misleading impression as to the effects of alcohol consumption on health. TTB will evaluate such statements on a case-by-case basis and may require as part of the health-related statement a disclaimer, or some other qualifying statement, to dispel any misleading impression conveyed by the health-related statement. Such disclaimer or other qualifying statement must appear as prominent as the health-related statement.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Tax Classification of Extraction Products</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu7" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep7" name="ep7"></a>EP7: How will TTB classify extraction products that are considered alcohol beverages?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub7" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The tax classification of extraction products under the IRC will depend on the formulation and method of production. The alcohol used in all of the extraction products tested by TTB was distilled spirits, and these FAQs address distilled spirits in greater detail than wine or beer. However, it is possible that extraction products could be produced with alcohol derived from fermentation, such as wine or beer. If distilled spirits are used in the formulation of the extraction product it will likely be classified as a distilled spirit. If wine is used in the formulation of the extraction product it will likely be classified as a wine and likewise, if beer is used in the formulation of the extraction product it will likely be classified as a beer.</p> 
            <p>Producers of extraction products may submit to the <a href="/offices/alcohol-labeling-and-formulation-division">Alcohol Labeling and Formulation Division</a> a formula providing detailed information about the ingredients used in their product, and the method of production. This is necessary in order for us to advise producers about the classification of their products, possible qualification requirements, and potential labeling requirements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Bringing the Producers of Extraction Products into Compliance</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu8" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep8" name="ep8"></a>EP8: What requirements apply to my production of extraction products as alcohol beverages?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub8" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you produce extraction products that contain 0.5 percent or more ABV and are found to be fit for beverage purposes, you are required to qualify as an alcohol beverage producer. Depending on the type of alcohol used in producing the extraction product you must <a href="/distilled-spirits/permits">qualify as a distilled spirits plant</a>, <a href="/wine/permits">qualify as a wine premises</a>, or <a href="/beer/ttb-beer-brewers-notice">qualify as a brewer</a> under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=ae3312b52c19993f69af6029957ec49f&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr19_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 19</a>, <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=506cf0c03546efff958847134c5527d3&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a> or <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi/t/text/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=e308c59ce106f7086e7b13c5b176d374&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.20&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 25</a> of the TTB regulations respectively. Extraction products that are determined to be alcohol beverages are also subject to excise taxes.</p> 
            <p>For example, if the extraction product is classified as distilled spirits, the regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=fe03bb919c4e3151ea5d84aa47d57dda&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.15.14.198.6&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 19.348</a> require the proprietor to file a formula on <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510051.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Form 5100.51</a> that includes a quantitative list of ingredients and a description of the method of manufacture or process of production used to produce the spirits. You must remove your distilled spirits from your premises in containers that comply with the requirements for containers and marks under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=fe03bb919c4e3151ea5d84aa47d57dda&amp;rgn=div6&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.15.19&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 19, subpart S.</a> The product also will be subject to FAA Act requirements and the labeling and advertising requirements of <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=0b1336cf26cbf0cd359ebd43d02b8706&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.3&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 5</a>, including standard liquor bottles and standards of fill.</p> 
            <p>Similar requirements under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi/t/text/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=e308c59ce106f7086e7b13c5b176d374&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.20&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 25</a> will apply if the extraction product is classified as a beer and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=d473aa9e89b3f7135e18c8da635f26aa&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr7_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 7</a> will also apply if the extraction product is classified as a <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=52ecf7a13d490a737ea0ff3f2d0d0615&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.5.2.41.1&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">malt beverage</a> as defined in the regulations. If the extraction product is classified as a wine, requirements under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=506cf0c03546efff958847134c5527d3&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a> will apply and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=506cf0c03546efff958847134c5527d3&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a> will apply if the extraction product is classified as a wine and has 7 percent or more ABV. This includes requirements for standard wine containers and standards of fill.</p> 
            <p>Distilled spirits and wines with 7 percent or more ABV are also subject to the standards of fill requirements at 27 CFR <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=0b1336cf26cbf0cd359ebd43d02b8706&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.3&amp;idno=27#27:1.0.1.1.3.6.25.4" target="_blank" rel="noopener">5.47a</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=506cf0c03546efff958847134c5527d3&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;idno=27#27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.3" target="_blank" rel="noopener">4.72</a> respectively.</p> 
            <p>In addition, as previously noted, if your extraction product contains 0.5 percent or more ABV, your containers must bear the health warning statement, as outlined under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi/t/text/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=fd8b0ad16b93584273aefb7460a98eb4&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr16_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="EPmenu9" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep9" name="ep9"></a>EP9: How do I qualify as an alcohol beverage producer?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub9" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Learn about <a href="/distilled-spirits/permits">qualifying as a distilled spirits producer</a> or contact TTB's Distilled Spirits Applications Section toll free at 877-882-3277.</p> 
            <p>Learn about <a href="/beer/ttb-beer-brewers-notice">qualifying as a brewer</a> or contact TTB's Brewery Applications Section toll free at 877-882-3277.</p> 
            <p>Learn about <a href="/wine/federal-application-process">qualifying a wine premises</a> or contact TTB's Winery Applications Section toll free at 877-882-3277.</p> 
            <p>You also should contact <a href="/wine/alcohol-beverage-control-boards">State and local alcohol regulatory authorities</a> to learn about State and local requirements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 06/10/2014</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="EPmenu10" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep10" name="ep10"></a>EP10: What Federal tax applies if my extraction product is an alcohol beverage?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub10" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The IRC at <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2011-title26/pdf/USCODE-2011-title26-subtitleE-chap51-subchapA-partI-subpartA-sec5001.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">26 U.S.C. 5001</a> imposes on all distilled spirits produced in or imported into the United States a tax at the rate of $13.50 on each proof gallon and a proportionate tax at the like rate on all fractional parts of a proof gallon.</p> 
            <p>Information about taxes on wine can be found at <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2009-title26/pdf/USCODE-2009-title26-subtitleE-chap51-subchapA-partI-subpartC-sec5041.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">26 U.S.C. 5041</a>, and information about taxes on beer can be found at <a id="k19" name="k14"></a><a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2010-title26/html/USCODE-2010-title26-subtitleE-chap51-subchapA-partI-subpartD-sec5051.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener">26 U.S.C. 5051</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Importers, Wholesalers and Retailers of Extraction Products</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu11" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep11" name="ep11"></a>EP11: What responsibilities do importers, wholesalers, and retailers have regarding extraction products that are determined to be alcohol beverages?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub11" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If the extraction product is a wine, malt beverage, or distilled spirits product subject to the FAA Act, importers and wholesalers of the product must <a href="/applications">obtain a basic permit from TTB</a>. Additionally, wholesalers and retailers of extraction products that are alcohol beverages must register with TTB as dealers in alcohol beverages, using form <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f56305d.pdf" target="_blank">TTB F 5630.5d</a>. Dealers must meet the requirements outlined under the TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi/t/text/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;sid=a0e25d4da42308ec77cd9c35340bfef3&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.26&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 31</a>. Dealers also should contact <a href="/wine/alcohol-beverage-control-boards">State and local alcohol regulatory authorities</a> to learn about State and local requirements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Determining Whether an Extraction Product is Unfit for Beverage Purposes</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu12" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep12" name="ep12"></a>EP12: How does TTB determine whether or not a product is unfit for beverage purposes?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub12" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB's <a href="/scientific-services-division/form-5154-1-resources">Nonbeverage Products Laboratory</a> has developed several tools and guidelines that limit subjectivity in determining if a product is unfit for beverage purposes.</p> 
            <p>To determine if an extraction product is unfit for beverage purposes, TTB may review the ingredients and formulation taking into account the presence of any flavor chemicals or other ingredients that would make the product unfit for beverage purposes.</p> 
            <p>Due to the extensive guidelines for nonbeverage product formulation, employing organoleptic tasting protocols to determine unfitness for beverage purposes is very rare. When used, it is for the purpose of determining whether the average person would mistake the product for an alcoholic beverage.</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>See our guidance on how you can be certain your product is <a href="/scientific-services-division/dbmenu3" target="_self">unfit for beverage purposes</a>.</li> 
             <li>See our <a href="/scientific-services-division/dbmenu3sub1" target="_self">tools</a> to help you decide which set of guidelines is relevant to your product.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 06/10/2014</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Ensuring that Extraction Products will not be Considered Alcohol Beverages</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu13" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep13" name="ep13"></a>EP13: How can I ensure that my extraction product will not be considered an alcohol beverage?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub13" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>One way to ensure that an extraction product will not be considered an alcohol beverage is to reduce the alcohol content of the product to less than 0.5 percent ABV in the final container used at retail either by diluting the product or eliminating the use of alcohol in the production process, either as an extraction agent or preservative.</p> 
            <p>If the alcohol content of the extraction product is 0.5 percent ABV or above and the extraction product is found to be fit for beverage purposes it must be reformulated to make it unfit for beverage purposes in order not to be considered an alcohol beverage. See FAQ EP12 for additional information on how products can be made unfit for beverage purposes.</p> 
            <p>If you have any questions about whether your product is unfit for beverage purposes, please contact TTB's <a href="/scientific-services-division/form-5154-1-resources">Nonbeverage Products Laboratory</a> at 2402641589 or <a href="/contact-ssd">Submit Inquiry</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/12/2013</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <h3>Tax Drawback for Alcohol used in Producing Extraction Products</h3> 
          <div id="EPmenu14" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ep14" name="ep14"></a>EP14: Can I file a claim for the tax paid on the alcohol used to produce my extraction products if they are unfit for beverage purposes?
          </div> 
          <div id="EPsub14" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Drawback is the portion of excise taxes returned to the manufacturer of nonbeverage products when the tax-paid on the distilled spirits is used to produce approved products unfit for beverage purposes. If you use distilled spirits on which tax was paid to produce your extraction products, you may be able to apply for a drawback of a portion of those taxes. To apply for tax drawback you must submit a formula on <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f51541.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Form 5154.1</a>. Upon approval of the formula the manufacturer may file a claim on <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f51542.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Form 5154.2</a> with the National Revenue Center to drawback the excise tax.</p> 
            <p>See more information on <a href="/scientific-services-division/nonbeverage-drawback-alcohol">nonbeverage drawback alcohol</a>.</p> 
            <p>Drawback of tax only applies to domestically produced products. No drawback of tax will be allowed on imported products, and no drawback is allowed for taxpaid wine or beer.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 06/10/2014</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p><a href="#content">Return to Top </a></p> 
         <p>&nbsp;</p> 
         <div id="print_cider" class="print_cider">
          <a id="Cider" name="Cider"></a> 
          <p id="printIcon" class="printIcon" align="right"><a onclick="printContent('print_cider')" href="#Cider"><img src="/images/printer.gif" alt="Print" width="24" height="24" align="top" border="0" hspace="4">Print</a></p> 
          <h3>Cider</h3> 
          <div id="importantFooter"> 
           <div> 
            <p><strong><img src="/images/important.png" alt="Yellow icon with a black exclamation mark." width="29" height="29" align="absmiddle"> IMPORTANT<br> CHANGES TO THE CRITERIA FOR THE HARD CIDER TAX RATE</strong></p> 
            <p>The Internal Revenue Code criteria for the "hard cider" tax rate have changed for wines removed from wine premises or customs custody on or after January 1, 2017. The hard cider tax rate is lower than the tax rate for other wines. The modified criteria broaden the range of wines eligible for the hard cider tax rate as follows:</p> 
            <ul type="disc"> 
             <li>The allowable alcohol content increases from less than 7 percent to less than (not equal to) 8.5 percent alcohol by volume;</li> 
             <li>The allowable carbonation level increases from 0.392 to 0.64 grams of carbon dioxide per hundred milliliters of wine; and</li> 
             <li>The use of pears and pear juice concentrate is authorized in wine eligible for the hard cider tax rate.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Wine eligible for the hard cider tax rate cannot contain fruit products or fruit flavors other than apple and pear.</p> The information in these <a href="#Cider">FAQs</a> may not reflect these changes. TTB will update it as soon as possible. In the meantime, please see <a href="/industry-circulars/ttb-industry-circulars-17-2">TTB Industry Circular 20172</a> for more explanation of the modified criteria for the hard cider tax rate.&nbsp; The Industry Circular also provides information on the application of the small wine producers' tax credit, formula requirements, and carbonation testing and recordkeeping requirements.&nbsp;
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu1" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c1" name="c1"></a>CID1: What is "cider"?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub1" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>When used generally in these FAQs, the terms "cider" and "hard cider" refer to wine fermented from apples (including apple juice or concentrate). However, the terms have different meanings under the Internal Revenue Code of 1986, as amended (IRC) and the Federal Alcohol Administration Act (FAA Act), as set forth further in these FAQs.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu2" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c2" name="c2"></a>CID2: What products may be labeled as "cider" under the regulations implementing the Federal Alcohol Administration (FAA) Act?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub2" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The labeling regulations for wine set forth standards of identity for various classes of wine, including fruit wine. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c5d6bb69755cc32f0c9029445a86cf17&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;idno=27">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a>.) Under these regulations, "apple wine" or "cider" is a fruit wine produced by the normal alcoholic fermentation of the juice of sound, ripe apples. Cider must be derived wholly (except for sugar, water, or added alcohol) from apples.</p> 
            <p>The labeling of cider is subject to the regulations in 27 CFR part 4 only if the wine contains not less than 7 percent and not more than 24 percent alcohol by volume. Additional information about the standards of identity for cider and other fruit wines is contained in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c5d6bb69755cc32f0c9029445a86cf17&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;idno=27">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu3" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c3" name="c3"></a>CID3: Do I need to obtain a Certificate of Label Approval (COLA) for my cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub3" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The FAA Act generally requires that a bottler obtain a <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510031.pdf" target="_blank">certificate of label approval</a> (COLA) from TTB prior to bottling wine. A bottler may obtain a <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510031.pdf" target="_blank">certificate of exemption</a> from label approval from TTB upon establishing that the wine will not be sold, shipped, or otherwise introduced in interstate or foreign commerce. Importers are required to obtain a COLA from TTB prior to removing wine in containers from customs custody for consumption.</p> 
            <p>The FAA Act and its implementing regulations do not apply to wine that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume; thus, cider with an alcohol content of less than 7 percent alcohol by volume is not subject to the COLA requirement. However, please note that all wine removed from wine premises is subject to TTB wine labeling requirements contained in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=ee0120a170c6ea39134c9e711d9affc9&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.14&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27&nbsp;CFR 24.257</a> (or <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c77895f7200a224f5f91c5efff1f1f59&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.16&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.259</a> for containers larger than 4 liters). (See <a href="#c4">CID 4</a> and <a href="#c5">CID 5</a> for more information about the labeling of wines containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu4" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c4" name="c4"></a>CID4: Do Food and Drug Administration (FDA) food labeling requirements apply to cider containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub4" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. While a TTB COLA is <em>not</em> required for wines containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, these wines must comply with applicable <a href="https://www.fda.gov/Food/GuidanceRegulation/GuidanceDocumentsRegulatoryInformation/LabelingNutrition/ucm2006828.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener">FDA food labeling requirements</a>, including ingredient labeling, nutrient labeling, and allergen labeling requirements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu5" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c5" name="c5"></a>CID5: Is a health warning statement required on the container of an apple wine (cider) containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub5" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. All alcohol beverages containing 0.5 percent or more alcohol by volume must be labeled with the health warning statement required by <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2013-title27/pdf/USCODE-2013-title27-chap8-subchapII-sec215.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 U.S.C. 215</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu6" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c6" name="c6"></a>CID6: Under the Federal Alcohol Administration (FAA) Act labeling regulations, is there a difference between "apple wine" and "cider"?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub6" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The labeling regulations allow either the term "apple wine" or "cider" as the designation for apple wine that complies with the standards of identity set forth in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=4ad7d635a75055f1ebe5c20e4506bf82&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)(5)</a>. You may also designate this product as "apple cider." Furthermore, the terms may be used interchangeably as part of a statement of composition for a wine specialty product (for example, "apple wine with natural blueberry flavor" or "cider with natural blueberry flavor").</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu7" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c7" name="c7"></a>CID7: What are the labeling requirements for cider that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub7" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The label must meet the requirements of <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=599804bef7b2f8300cecf22442b27af0&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR parts 4</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">16</a>. This includes, but is not limited to, the following mandatory information on the label:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Brand name;</li> 
             <li>Class or type designation;</li> 
             <li>Name and address of the bottler or importer, as applicable;</li> 
             <li>Net contents of the container;</li> 
             <li>Alcohol content;</li> 
             <li>Sulfite statement, if applicable (Contains Sulfites); and</li> 
             <li>Health Warning Statement.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Other labeling statements may be required, in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_132&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.32</a>. Furthermore, all wines must be labeled and marked in accordance with IRC requirements in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;n=pt27.1.24&amp;r=PART&amp;ty=HTML" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a> (for domestic wines), <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr26_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 26</a> (for wines coming into the United States from Puerto Rico or the Virgin Islands) or <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr27_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 27</a> (for imported wines).</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu8" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c8" name="c8"></a>CID8: May I label my wine made exclusively from pears as "pear cider"?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub8" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If the pear wine contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume and is made exclusively (except for sugar, water, or added alcohol) from pears, it may not be labeled as "pear cider." As noted in <a href="#c2">CID2</a>, wines designated as "cider" under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c5d6bb69755cc32f0c9029445a86cf17&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a> must be derived wholly (except for sugar, water, or added alcohol) from apples. The regulations provide that wine that is derived wholly (except for sugar, water, or added alcohol) from pears must be designated as either "pear wine" or as "perry."</p> 
            <p>The labeling of perry is subject to the regulations in part 4 only if the wine contains not less than 7 percent and not more than 24 percent alcohol by volume. Additional information about the standards of identity for perry and other fruit wines are contained in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c5d6bb69755cc32f0c9029445a86cf17&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu9" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c9" name="c9"></a>CID9: How do I designate a wine fermented from both apple juice and pear juice, where the wine has not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub9" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the regulations implementing the FAA Act, a wine produced from the fermentation of apple juice and pear juice must be designated&nbsp;with a truthful and adequate statement of composition such as "apple-pear wine," "apple perry," or "pear cider." The designation "fruit wine" may appear in direct conjunction with the statement of composition. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c5d6bb69755cc32f0c9029445a86cf17&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a> for more information.)</p> 
            <p>Please note that this type of wine does not require formula approval as long as it is produced in accordance with the rules for natural fruit wine under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c397f2143c2d893be95e3a46accd6b43&amp;node=pt27.1.24&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu10" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c10" name="c10"></a>CID10: How do I designate a wine fermented from both apple juice and blueberry juice, where the wine has not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub10" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the regulations implementing the FAA Act, a wine produced from the fermentation of apple juice and blueberry juice must be designated&nbsp;with a truthful and adequate statement of composition such as "apple-blueberry wine" or "blueberry cider." The designation "fruit wine" may appear in direct conjunction with the statement of composition. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c5d6bb69755cc32f0c9029445a86cf17&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a> for more information.)</p> 
            <p>Please note that this type of wine does not require formula approval as long as it is produced in accordance with the rules for natural fruit wine under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c397f2143c2d893be95e3a46accd6b43&amp;node=pt27.1.24&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu11" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c11" name="c11"></a>CID11: How do I designate a wine made from blending apple wine with pear wine, where the wine has not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub11" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the regulations implementing the FAA Act, a wine made from blending apple wine with pear wine must be designated with a statement of composition under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_134&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.34</a>(a), such as "apple wine - pear wine" or "cider - perry." The wine may also be labeled with a fanciful name (such as "apple pear delight"). The fanciful name "apple perry" or "pear cider" would not be approved because it is misleading as to the identity of the product.</p> 
            <p>Please note that this type of wine would require formula approval as an "other than standard" wine under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=1124e739baf10710c28294bd3f979e71&amp;node=se27.1.24_1211&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.211</a>. (See <a href="#c28">CID28</a> for information about obtaining formula approval.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu12" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c12" name="c12"></a>CID12: How do I designate a wine made from blending apple wine with blueberry wine, where the wine has not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub12" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the regulations implementing the FAA Act, a wine made from blending apple wine with blueberry wine must be designated with a statement of composition under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_134&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.34</a>(a), such as "apple wine - blueberry wine" or "cider and blueberry wine." The wine may also be labeled with a fanciful name (such as "apple blueberry delight"). The fanciful name "blueberry cider" would not be approved because it is misleading as to the identity of the product.</p> 
            <p>Please note that this type of wine would require formula approval as an "other than standard" wine under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=1124e739baf10710c28294bd3f979e71&amp;node=se27.1.24_1211&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.211</a>. (See <a href="#c28">CID28</a> for information about obtaining formula approval.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu13" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c13" name="c13"></a>CID13: May I add flavoring materials, such as honey, spices, natural flavors, or artificial flavors, to my cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub13" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you add flavoring materials to apple wine, the resulting product may be a special natural wine or an "other than standard" wine under the Internal Revenue Code (IRC), depending on how it is formulated. A proprietor of wine premises must obtain formula approval before producing either type of wine, even if the wine has less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. (See <a href="#c28">CID28</a> for information about formulas and <a href="#c14">CID14</a> for information on the labeling of flavored wines.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu14" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c14" name="c14"></a>CID14: How do I designate a wine made from apple wine that has been flavored with honey, spices, natural flavors or artificial flavors?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub14" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the labeling regulations that implement the FAA Act, this type of wine may not be designated as simply "cider" because it no longer meets the standards of identity set forth in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=4ad7d635a75055f1ebe5c20e4506bf82&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)</a>. Instead, it must be labeled with a statement of composition in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=4ad7d635a75055f1ebe5c20e4506bf82&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.3&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27&nbsp;CFR 4.22(a)</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=1124e739baf10710c28294bd3f979e71&amp;node=se27.1.4_134&amp;rgn=div8">27 CFR 4.34</a>. As part of the formula approval, TTB will provide a suggested statement of composition for the product designation, such as "cider with artificial and natural blueberry flavors" or "apple cider with honey and spices." If you wish, you may also label the wine with a fanciful name but this name may not be misleading as to the identity of the product. Accordingly, the fanciful name "blueberry cider" would not be approved for an apple wine with natural blueberry flavor.</p> 
            <p>If your flavored wines contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, the labeling regulations in 27 CFR part 4 do not apply; instead, <a href="https://www.fda.gov/Food/GuidanceRegulation/GuidanceDocumentsRegulatoryInformation/LabelingNutrition/ucm2006828.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener">FDA food labeling rules</a> apply.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu15" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c15" name="c15"></a>CID15: May I label my cider as "hard cider" even if it doesn't qualify for the hard cider tax rate under 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6)?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub15" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. If your wine has not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, and it complies with the standards of identity for "cider" under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=4ad7d635a75055f1ebe5c20e4506bf82&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;rgn=div8">27 CFR 4.21(e)(5)</a>, it may be labeled as either "cider" or "hard cider." Pursuant to <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=1124e739baf10710c28294bd3f979e71&amp;r=SECTION&amp;n=se27.1.4_132" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.32</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=1124e739baf10710c28294bd3f979e71&amp;r=SECTION&amp;n=se27.1.4_136" target="_blank" rel="noopener">4.36</a>, the wine must also be labeled with a numerical statement of alcohol content (unless it has no more than 14 percent alcohol by volume and it is designated as a "fruit table wine" or an "apple table wine") - this labeling information will adequately convey that the product is not eligible for the "hard cider" tax rate.</p> 
            <p>If your wine is not subject to the FAA Act labeling requirements, it must still be labeled in accordance with the regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f520c388fa84b8b503288c532da8422a&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.14&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.257(a)(4)(iv)</a> before removal from wine premises. These regulations require the wine to be labeled with the class or type of wine or with a statement of composition that, when viewed with the alcohol content, gives enough information to adequately identify the tax class of the wine. For example, a still wine that is labeled "hard cider with natural raspberry flavor" and "6 percent alcohol by volume" is adequately marked to identify the wine's tax class, because the raspberry flavor makes it ineligible for the hard cider tax rate.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu16" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c16" name="c16"></a>CID16: Do special labeling requirements apply to effervescent cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub16" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. An effervescent wine is a wine that contains more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters. See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=15ecb5b77b8d6f1f61b54155027a5353&amp;node=se27.1.24_110&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.10</a>. Under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=4ad7d635a75055f1ebe5c20e4506bf82&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(e)(5)</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f520c388fa84b8b503288c532da8422a&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.14&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.257</a>, effervescent cider must be labeled as "sparkling" or "carbonated," as appropriate. (See <a href="#c17">CID17</a> for more information.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu17" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c17" name="c17"></a>CID17: What is the difference between "sparkling" cider and "carbonated" cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub17" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>A "sparkling wine" is made effervescent (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters of wine) by carbon dioxide resulting solely from the secondary fermentation of the wine within a closed container, tank or bottle. A sparkling cider must be labeled either as "sparkling cider" or "sparkling apple wine." A wine made effervescent in any other way (such as injection of carbon dioxide) is considered artificially carbonated, and must be labeled as "carbonated" (for example, "carbonated cider" or "carbonated apple wine").</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu18" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c18" name="c18"></a>CID18: Are effervescent wines subject to different tax rates from still wines?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub18" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the IRC, sparkling and artificially carbonated wines are subject to higher rates of tax than still wine. (See <a href="#c21">CID21</a> for more information about the tax rates.)</p> 
            <p>Furthermore, sparkling and carbonated ciders are not eligible for the hard cider tax rate provided by 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6) or for the exemption from tax provided by 26 U.S.C. 5042(a)(1). (See <a href="#c23">CID23</a> and <a href="#c25">CID25</a> for more information.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu19" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c19" name="c19"></a>CID19: May I label my cider with an appellation of origin?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub19" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=d84c5de209aad5e993292071a71fb9c4&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.6&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.25</a> permit fruit wine (including cider) to be labeled with an appellation of origin such as a country, State or county, assuming that certain conditions are met. However, only grape wine may be labeled with an appellation that is a viticultural area.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu20" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c20" name="c20"></a>CID20: Do TTB's standards of fill apply to cider? May I sell my cider in 12 fl. oz. bottles or in kegs?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub20" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB's standards of fill apply to wine (including cider) that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=75d7e3b7dd2080a4286690269e96ee13&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.3&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.72</a>. Twelve fluid ounces (12 fl. oz.) is not an approved standard of fill for wine. Producers, importers and wholesalers are prohibited from selling or shipping, or delivering for sale or shipment, or otherwise introducing in interstate commerce, wine that is not bottled or packed in an authorized standard of fill. See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=75d7e3b7dd2080a4286690269e96ee13&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.1&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.70</a>.</p> 
            <p>You may use kegs as long as they conform to one of the standards of fill. However, wine packed in containers of 18 liters or more does not need to comply with these standards of fill requirements - see <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=75d7e3b7dd2080a4286690269e96ee13&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.1&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.70(b)(2)</a>.</p> 
            <p>If the cider contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, the standards of fill in 27 CFR part 4 do not apply.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu21" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c21" name="c21"></a>CID21: What are the Federal excise tax rates for cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub21" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Ciders that are not subject to the special rules set forth in <a href="#c23">CID23</a> or <a href="#c25">CID25</a> are taxed at the appropriate <a href="/what-we-do/taxes-and-filing/tax-rates">wine excise tax</a> rate. The following are the basic tax classification categories for wine:</p> 
            <p>Still wine (containing not more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters):</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Not more than 14 percent alcohol by volume: $1.07 per wine gallon</li> 
             <li>Over 14 and not more than 21 percent alcohol by volume: $1.57 per wine gallon</li> 
             <li>Over 21 and not more than 24 percent alcohol by volume: $3.15 per wine gallon</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Effervescent wine (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters):</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Artificially Carbonated Wine: $3.30 per wine gallon</li> 
             <li>Sparkling Wine: $3.40 per wine gallon</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>In addition, domestic wine producers may be eligible for a small producer tax credit based on their volume of production, as described in <a href="#c26">CID26</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu22" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c22" name="c22"></a>CID22: For tax purposes, what is the difference between "sparkling" cider and "artificially carbonated" cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub22" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>A "sparkling wine" is made effervescent (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters of wine) by carbon dioxide resulting solely from the secondary fermentation of the wine within a closed container, tank or bottle. A wine made effervescent in any other way (such as injection of carbon dioxide) is considered artificially carbonated. (See <a href="#c16">CID16</a> and <a href="#c17">CID17</a> for information about the labeling of these products.)</p> 
            <p>Please note that both sparkling ciders and carbonated ciders are <em>not</em> <em>eligible</em> for the hard cider tax rate provided by 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6) or for the exemption from tax provided by 26 U.S.C. 5042(a)(1). (See <a href="#c23">CID23</a> and <a href="#c25">CID25</a> for more information.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu23" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c23" name="c23"></a>CID23: What are the tax rates for "hard cider" under 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6)?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub23" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The IRC provides a tax rate of 22.6 per wine gallon for "hard cider" that meets specific statutory criteria. To qualify for this tax rate, hard cider must be a still wine derived primarily from apples or apple concentrate and water, containing no other fruit product, and containing at least 0.5 percent and less than 7 percent alcohol by volume.&nbsp; (See 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6))</p> 
            <p>The regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=15ecb5b77b8d6f1f61b54155027a5353&amp;node=se27.1.24_110&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.10</a> set forth a definition of "hard cider" that is eligible for the hard cider tax rate of 22.6 per gallon. Under the law and regulations, cider must meet <em>all</em> of the following criteria in order to qualify for the "hard cider" tax rate:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>The cider must be a still wine. This means it must contain no more than 0.392 gram of carbon dioxide per hundred milliliters of wine. Effervescent cider (including sparkling and carbonated cider) is <em>not</em> eligible for the hard cider tax rate;</li> 
             <li>The wine must be derived primarily from apples or apple concentrate and water. This means that apple juice, or the equivalent amount of concentrate reconstituted to the original brix of the juice prior to concentration, must represent more than 50 percent of the volume of the finished product;</li> 
             <li>The wine must contain no other fruit product (such as juice from a fruit other than apples); nor may it contain any artificial product that imparts a fruit flavor other than apple (such as an artificial pear or blueberry flavor);</li> 
             <li>The wine must contain at least 0.5 percent but less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. Any wine containing 7 percent or more alcohol by volume is not eligible for the hard cider tax rate;</li> 
             <li>The wine must have the taste, aroma, and characteristics generally attributed to hard cider; and</li> 
             <li>The wine must be sold or offered for sale as hard cider.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>(See <a href="#c15">CID15</a> for information about using the designation "hard cider" on labels.) <a id="c32" name="c24"></a></p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/10/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu24" class="mmenu">
           <a></a>CID24: I add artificial raspberry flavor to my cider, which has an alcohol content of 6 percent alcohol by volume. Does the addition of this flavor affect the cider's eligibility for the hard cider tax rate?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub24" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. Because the IRC provides that the hard cider tax rate under 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6) is not available to wines that contain a fruit product other than apple, a cider containing either natural or artificial fruit flavors (other than apple flavors) is not eligible for the hard cider tax rate of 22.6 per gallon. (See <a href="#c23">CID23</a>.) Instead, a fruit-flavored cider would be taxed at the appropriate wine excise tax rate. (See <a href="#c21">CID21</a>.)</p> 
            <p>Please note that non-fruit flavors such as spices or honey would not affect a cider's eligibility for the hard cider tax rate. You will need formula approval for this product because flavors have been added. (See <a href="#c28">CID28</a>.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu25" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c25" name="c25"></a>CID25: What type of cider is exempt from Federal excise taxes on wine under 26 U.S.C. 5042(a)(1)?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub25" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The IRC provides a tax exemption for certain ciders as outlined in 26 U.S.C. 5042(a)(1) and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=03a49de108468b2eef6477fcc23fe228&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.3.332.36&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.76</a>. This is an extremely limited exemption that was intended to allow an exemption from the wine tax for farmers and growers who produced fermented cider for sale at roadside stands.</p> 
            <p>To qualify for this exemption, cider must meet <em>all</em> of the following criteria:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>The cider must be produced solely from the noneffervescent fermentation of apple juice. Sugars, syrups or concentrate may not be added to tax exempt cider. Any cider that is effervescent (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 ml) is <em>not </em>eligible for the exemption from tax;</li> 
             <li>The cider must be made without the use of any preservative methods or materials (such as the use of pasteurization or the addition of sulfites);</li> 
             <li>The cider must be produced at a place other than a bonded wine premises; and</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>The cider must be sold or offered for sale as cider and not as wine or a substitute for wine.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu26" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c26" name="c26"></a>CID26: Are cider producers eligible for the small producer tax credit?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub26" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Like other domestic wine producers, domestic cider producers are eligible for the small producer tax credit under 26 U.S.C. 5041(c) if they produce not more than 250,000 gallons of wine during a calendar year. The credit may be taken on the first 100,000 gallons of wine removed for consumption or sale by an eligible person during a calendar year, and it does not apply to sparkling wine (including sparkling cider) or to wine that is not produced at a qualified bonded wine premises in the United States. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f5749a5eb03dd06791b6529e60f3841e&amp;node=se27.1.24_1278&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.278</a> for more details about the conditions for taking the credit.) For additional information on computing the tax credit, please see TTB's <a href="/tax-audit/quick-reference-guide-to-wine-excise-tax" target="_blank">Quick Reference Guide to Wine Excise Tax</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu27" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c27" name="c27"></a>CID27: How much is the small producer tax credit for hard cider?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub27" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>An eligible producer of "hard cider" that is subject to the tax rate under 26 U.S.C. 5041(b)(6) may claim a credit of up to 5.6 per wine gallon. An eligible producer of cider that does not qualify as "hard cider" can claim a credit of up to 90 per gallon. In either case, the credit is reduced for persons who produce more than 150,000 gallons of wine during the calendar year. As noted in <a href="#c26">CID26</a>, sparkling cider is not eligible for a credit, and other conditions apply. For additional information on computing the tax credit, please see TTB's <a href="/tax-audit/quick-reference-guide-to-wine-excise-tax" target="_blank">Quick Reference Guide to Wine Excise Tax</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu28" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c28" name="c28"></a>CID28: Do I need to obtain TTB formula approval before producing cider or other fruit wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub28" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>No formula approval is required to produce natural cider or other natural fruit wine, even if the wine is fermented from the juice of different fruits. A "natural" wine is the product of the juice or must of sound, ripe fruit (including berries) made with any cellar treatment authorized by Subparts F and L of part 24 and containing not more than 21 percent by weight (21 degrees Brix dealcoholized wine) of total solids. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=15ecb5b77b8d6f1f61b54155027a5353&amp;node=se27.1.24_110&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.10</a>.) However, TTB formula approval is required for:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Wine produced by blending two finished fruit wines together (for example, apple wine and pear wine);</li> 
             <li>Wine with added flavors or spices; and</li> 
             <li>Wine made with excess sugar and/or water or other cellar treatments not authorized for natural wine in Subparts F and L of part 24.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Additional information about obtaining TTB formula approval may be found on TTB's <a href="/formulation">formulation Web page</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu29" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c29" name="c29"></a>CID29: What IRC labeling requirements apply to all cider and other wine removed from wine premises?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub29" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Although TTB's FAA Act wine labeling regulations in part 4 do not apply to wine that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, IRC labeling requirements and TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=ee0120a170c6ea39134c9e711d9affc9&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.14&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.257</a> apply to all wines that are removed from wine premises. Under these regulations, labels must include the following information:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Name and address of the wine premises;</li> 
             <li>Brand name;</li> 
             <li>Alcohol content;</li> 
             <li>Net contents of the container; and</li> 
             <li>Kind of wine, which means -</li> 
             <li> 
              <ul> 
               <li>Class or type in accordance with 27 CFR Part 4; or</li> 
               <li>An adequate statement of composition for wines not subject to part 4. Statements of composition must include enough information to identify the tax class when viewed with the alcohol content.</li> 
              </ul> </li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Containers larger than 4 liters removed from the winery for consumption or sale must be labeled with this information as required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6e45cd360029c5f4828200781959393&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.16&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.259</a>. The required information may be cut, printed, or otherwise legibly and durably marked upon the container or placed on a label or tag securely affixed to the container.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu30" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c30" name="c30"></a>CID30: Do I need to obtain a permit from TTB to produce apple wine (cider) for commercial purposes?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub30" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. If you are producing wine (including cider) that is at least 0.5 percent alcohol by volume for commercial purposes, you must first establish winery premises, obtain a bond, and receive permission from TTB to operate. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f2797979c9c7cb4918f0569f0d09437f&amp;node=sp27.1.24.d&amp;rgn=div6" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24, subpart D</a>.) &nbsp;In addition, the FAA Act requires that anyone wishing to engage in the business of producing or blending wine (including cider) that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume must first obtain a basic permit from TTB. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr1_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 1</a>.) A basic permit under the FAA Act is not required for producers who only make wine that is less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. For more information on qualification requirements, <a href="/wine/federal-application-process">The Federal Application Process for the Wine Industry.</a></p> 
            <p>If you produce only tax-exempt cider that satisfies all of the requirements of 26 U.S.C. 5042(a)(1), and 27 CFR 24.76 you are not required to qualify as a bonded wine premises. (See <a href="#c25">CID25</a> for more information about these requirements.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu31" class="mmenu">
           <a id="c31" name="c31"></a>CID31: I import wine (including cider). What TTB requirements apply to my operations?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub31" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you import wine (including cider) that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, you must:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Obtain an <a href="/applications">Importer's Basic Permit</a> under the Federal Alcohol Administration Act;</li> 
             <li>Obtain a <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510031.pdf" target="_blank">certificate of label approval</a> for your cider;</li> 
             <li>Label the wine in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f6bd51e4d0db630348530e98808de566&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a>;</li> 
             <li>Label the wine with the health warning statement required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>;</li> 
             <li>Submit a pre-import letter (or formula) if required by Industry Circular 2007-4;</li> 
             <li>Pay the appropriate wine excise tax rate for the wine (see <a href="#c21">CID 21</a>); and</li> 
             <li>If required, obtain certification from the country of origin regarding the cellar treatment of the wine. (For more information, see our Web page on <a href="/wine/certification-requirements-for-imported-wine">certification requirements for imported natural wine</a>.)</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>If you also import wine (including cider) that contains at least 0.5 percent alcohol by volume and less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, the following requirements apply:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Label the wine in accordance with all applicable <a href="https://www.fda.gov/Food/GuidanceRegulation/GuidanceDocumentsRegulatoryInformation/LabelingNutrition/ucm2006828.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener">FDA food labeling requirements</a>;</li> 
             <li>Label the wine with the health warning statement required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>;</li> 
             <li>Pay the appropriate wine excise tax rate for the wine (see <a href="#c21">CID 21</a>);</li> 
             <li>Label the wine in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=91ce1e88410491fba85207705702e48e&amp;node=pt27.1.27&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 27</a>; and</li> 
             <li>If required, obtain certification from the country of origin regarding the cellar treatment of the wine. (For more information, see our Web page on <a href="/wine/certification-requirements-for-imported-wine">certification requirements for imported natural wine</a>.)</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>You do <em>not</em> need to obtain an Importer's Basic Permit if you import <em>only</em> wine that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. Additional information regarding the importation of alcohol beverages into the U.S. may be found on the <a href="/itd/importing-bottled-alcohol-beverages-into-the-united-states">International Affairs Division Web page</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/03/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="Cmenu32" class="mmenu">
           <a></a>CID32: How should I report kegs of cider and/or perry on my Report of Wine Premises Operations form (TTB F 5120.17)?
          </div> 
          <div id="Csub32" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Report kegs of cider and/or perry on your <a href="/forms/temporary-ttb-5120-17">Report of Wine Premises Operations</a> form (<a href="/forms/temporary-ttb-5120-17">TTB F 5120.17</a>) based on the container size. (Containers include bottles, cans, boxes, and kegs.) See wine FAQ <a href="/wine/wine-faqs#menu29">W29</a> for further guidance.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 05/10/2017</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p>
