         <div id="print_hw" class="print_hw">
          <a id="hw" name="hw"></a> 
          <p id="printIcon" class="printIcon" align="right"><a onclick="printContent('print_hw')" href="#hw"><img src="/images/printer.gif" alt="Print" width="24" height="24" align="top" border="0" hspace="4">Print</a></p> 
          <h3>Honey Wine (Mead)</h3> 
          <div id="HWmenu1" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw1" name="hw1"></a>HW1: What is "honey wine" under the IRC?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub1" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Honey wine is classified under the Internal Revenue Code of 1986, as amended (IRC), as an "agricultural wine." Agricultural wine is made from the fermentation of an agricultural product other than the juice of fruit. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.24_1200&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.200</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.24_1203&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">24.203</a>.) The production standards under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;n=pt27.1.24&amp;r=PART&amp;ty=HTML" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a> for honey wine apply only to domestic products.</p> 
            <p>The IRC does not allow for the use of coloring or flavoring materials (other than hops) in standard honey wine. (See <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2013-title26/pdf/USCODE-2013-title26-subtitleE-chap51-subchapF-partIII-sec5387.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">26 U.S.C. 5387</a> and <a href="#hw14">HW14</a> for more information.) Furthermore, wine spirits may not be added to standard honey wine, and standard honey wine may not contain more than 14 percent alcohol by volume. The IRC does provide for the production of wine specialty products that are made from a base of honey wine. These products are not standard agricultural wines, but are instead classified under the IRC regulations as "other than standard" (OTS) wines. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.24_1218&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.218.</a>)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu2" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw2" name="hw2"></a>HW2: What is "honey wine" under the FAA Act labeling regulations?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub2" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the regulations implementing the labeling provisions of the Federal Alcohol Administration Act (FAA Act), the standards of identity for wine made from the fermentation of agricultural products other than fruit are set forth in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_121&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(f)</a>. Pursuant to these regulations, a product designated as "honey wine" must be derived wholly (except for sugar, water, or added alcohol) from honey. Wines designated as "honey wine" under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=pt27.1.4&amp;rgn=div5">27 CFR part 4</a> also may contain hops, consistent with the levels set forth in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;n=pt27.1.24&amp;r=PART&amp;ty=HTML" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 24</a>. TTB allows the designation "mead" to be used in lieu of "honey wine." (See <a href="#hw6">HW6</a>.)</p> 
            <p>Certain wines fermented from honey that do not meet the standards of identity under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_121&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">&nbsp;4.21(f)</a> fall under the standards set forth in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_121&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(h)</a>, and thus must be designated as "imitation" or "other than standard" (OTS) wines. Other specialty products do not fall under any of the standards of identity in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=pt27.1.4&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 4</a> and must be designated with a truthful and adequate statement of composition. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_134&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.34(a)</a>.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu3" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw3" name="hw3"></a>HW3: Do I need to obtain a Certificate of Label Approval (COLA) for my mead?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub3" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The FAA Act generally requires that a bottler obtain a <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510031.pdf" target="_blank">certificate of label approval</a> (COLA) from TTB prior to bottling wine. A bottler may obtain a <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510031.pdf" target="_blank">certificate of exemption</a> from label approval from TTB upon establishing that the wine will not be sold, shipped, or otherwise introduced in interstate or foreign commerce. Importers are required to obtain a COLA from TTB prior to removing wine in containers from customs custody for consumption.</p> 
            <p>The FAA Act and its implementing regulations do not apply to wine that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume; thus mead with an alcohol content of less than 7 percent alcohol by volume is not subject to the COLA requirement. However, please note that all wine removed from wine premises is subject to TTB wine labeling requirements contained in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=ee0120a170c6ea39134c9e711d9affc9&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.14&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27&nbsp;CFR 24.257</a> (or <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c77895f7200a224f5f91c5efff1f1f59&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.16&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.259</a> for containers larger than 4 liters). (See <a href="#hw4">HW4</a> and <a href="#hw5">HW5</a> for more information about the labeling of these wines.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu4" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw4" name="hw4"></a>HW4: Do Food and Drug Administration (FDA) food labeling requirements apply to honey wine containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub4" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. While a TTB COLA is <em>not</em> required for wines containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, such wines must comply with applicable <a href="https://www.fda.gov/Food/GuidanceRegulation/GuidanceDocumentsRegulatoryInformation/LabelingNutrition/ucm2006828.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener">FDA food labeling requirements</a>, including ingredient labeling, nutrient labeling, and allergen labeling requirements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu5" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw5" name="hw5"></a>HW5: Is a health warning statement required on the container of a honey wine containing less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub5" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. All alcohol beverages containing 0.5 percent or more alcohol by volume must be labeled with the health warning statement required by <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2013-title27/pdf/USCODE-2013-title27-chap8-subchapII-sec215.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 U.S.C. 215</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu6" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw6" name="hw6"></a>HW6: Under the FAA Act labeling regulations, is there a difference between "honey wine" and "mead"?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub6" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>For labeling purposes, it is TTB's policy to allow either the term "honey wine" or "mead" as the designation for honey wine that complies with the standards of identity for honey wine set forth in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_121&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(f)</a>. Furthermore, the terms may be used interchangeably as part of a statement of composition for a wine specialty product (for example, "honey wine with natural cherry flavor" or "mead with natural cherry flavor").</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu7" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw7" name="hw7"></a>HW7: What are the labeling requirements for honey wine that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub7" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The label must meet the requirements of <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=599804bef7b2f8300cecf22442b27af0&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR parts 4</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">16</a>. This includes, but is not limited to, the following mandatory information on the label:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Brand name;</li> 
             <li>Class or type designation;</li> 
             <li>Name and address of the bottler or importer, as applicable;</li> 
             <li>Net contents of the container;</li> 
             <li>Alcohol content;</li> 
             <li>Sulfite statement, if applicable (Contains Sulfites); and</li> 
             <li>Health Warning Statement.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Other labeling statements may be required, in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_132&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.32</a>. Furthermore, all wines containing at least 0.5 percent alcohol by volume must be labeled and marked in accordance with IRC requirements in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;n=pt27.1.24&amp;r=PART&amp;ty=HTML" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24</a> (for domestic wines), <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr26_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 26</a> (for wines coming into the United States from Puerto Rico or the Virgin Islands) or <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr27_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 27</a> (for imported wines).</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu8" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw8" name="hw8"></a>HW8: How do I label a standard agricultural wine that is fermented from both honey and another agricultural product?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub8" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>A wine fermented from more than one agricultural product (for example, a wine that is fermented from both honey and rhubarb) may not be designated as "honey wine" or "mead" under TTB regulations. Instead, the wine must be designated with a truthful and adequate statement of composition, such as "rhubarb-honey wine" or "rhubarb mead." (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=a5906923bc094f0d2bf5224246f892be&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.2&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(f)(6)</a>.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu9" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw9" name="hw9"></a>HW9: How do I label a wine that is fermented from both honey and a fruit (such as cherry)?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub9" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>A wine fermented from both fruit juice and an agricultural product does not fall within any of the standards of identity in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;n=pt27.1.4&amp;r=PART&amp;ty=HTML" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a>. This type of wine must be designated with a truthful and adequate statement of composition in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_134&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.34</a>, such as "cherry-honey wine" or "cherry mead." The wine also may be labeled with a distinctive or fanciful name, such as "Cherry bee."</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu10" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw10" name="hw10"></a>HW10: What is the appropriate designation for a product consisting of honey wine blended with fruit wine (such as blueberry wine)?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub10" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>This product does not fall under any of the standards of identity in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/retrieveECFR?gp=&amp;SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;n=pt27.1.4&amp;r=PART&amp;ty=HTML" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a>, and thus must be labeled with a truthful and adequate statement of composition, such as "blueberry wine  honey wine," "blueberry wine and honey wine" or "blueberry wine  mead." In this instance, TTB would not approve a label with the term "blueberry mead" as either a fanciful name or as a designation because it would be misleading as to the identity of the wine.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu11" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw11" name="hw11"></a>HW11: Do special labeling requirements apply to effervescent honey wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub11" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. An effervescent wine is a wine that contains more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.24_110&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.10</a>.) Under <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_121&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(f)(6)</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.24_1257&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.257</a>, effervescent honey wine must be labeled as "sparkling" or "carbonated," as appropriate. (See <a href="#hw12">HW12</a> for more information.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu12" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw12" name="hw12"></a>HW12: What is the difference between "sparkling" honey wine and "carbonated" honey wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub12" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>A "sparkling wine" is made effervescent (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters of wine) by carbon dioxide resulting solely from the secondary fermentation of the wine within a closed container, tank or bottle. A sparkling honey wine must be labeled either as "sparkling mead" or "sparkling honey wine." A wine made effervescent in any other way (such as injection of carbon dioxide) is considered artificially carbonated, and must be labeled as "carbonated" (for example, "carbonated mead" or "carbonated honey wine").</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu13" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw13" name="hw13"></a>HW13: Are effervescent wines subject to different tax rates from still wines?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub13" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Under the IRC, sparkling and artificially carbonated wines are subject to higher rates of tax than still wine. (See <a href="#hw22">HW22</a> and <a href="#hw23">HW23</a> for more information about the tax rates).</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu14" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw14" name="hw14"></a>HW14: May I add coloring or flavoring materials to my honey wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub14" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you add coloring or flavoring materials (other than hops) to honey wine, the product is no longer a standard agricultural wine under the IRC. (See <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2013-title26/pdf/USCODE-2013-title26-subtitleE-chap51-subchapA-partI-subpartC-sec5041.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">26 U.S.C. 5387(b)</a> and <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=a5906923bc094f0d2bf5224246f892be&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.9.343.1&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.200</a>.) Furthermore, the product is no longer a "honey wine" under the FAA Act labeling regulations. However, you may produce a wine specialty product by adding flavoring and coloring materials to a base of honey wine. Because the product does not fall under any of the standards of identity set out in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr4_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a>, it must be designated with a truthful and adequate statement of composition, such as "honey wine with natural ginger flavor" or "mead with natural cherry flavor." The wine also may be labeled with a distinctive or fanciful name (such as "cherry honey delight"). In this case, TTB would not approve a label with a term such as "cherry mead" as either a fanciful name or a designation because it would be misleading as to the identity of the wine. However, a name such as "cherry-flavored mead" would be acceptable as the fanciful name, as long as an appropriate statement of composition appears as the designation.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu15" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw15" name="hw15"></a>HW15: May I label my honey wine with an appellation of origin?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub15" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=d84c5de209aad5e993292071a71fb9c4&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.3.25.6&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.25</a> permit wine (including an agricultural wine that falls under the standards of identity in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;node=se27.1.4_121&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.21(f)</a>) to be labeled with an appellation of origin such as a country, State or county, assuming that certain conditions are met. However, only grape wine may be labeled with an appellation that is a viticultural area.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu16" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw16" name="hw16"></a>HW16: Do TTB's standards of fill apply to mead? May I sell my mead in 12 fl. oz. bottles or in kegs?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub16" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB's standards of fill apply to wine (including mead) that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=75d7e3b7dd2080a4286690269e96ee13&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.3&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.72</a>.) Twelve fluid ounces (12 fluid oz.) is not an approved standard of fill for wine. Producers, importers, and wholesalers are prohibited from selling or shipping, or delivering for sale or shipment, or otherwise introducing in interstate commerce, wine that is not bottled or packed in an authorized standard of fill. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=75d7e3b7dd2080a4286690269e96ee13&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.1&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.70</a>.)</p> 
            <p>Wine may be packed in kegs as long as they conform to one of the standards of fill. However, wine packed in containers of 18 liters or more does not need to comply with these standards of fill requirements. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=75d7e3b7dd2080a4286690269e96ee13&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2.8.25.1&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 4.70(b)(2)</a>.)</p> 
            <p>If the wine contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, the standards of fill in 27 CFR part 4 do not apply. Thus, a mead that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume may be sold in 12 fl. oz. bottles.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu17" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw17" name="hw17"></a>HW17: What are the production standards under 27 CFR part 24 for standard honey wine produced in the United States?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub17" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=a5906923bc094f0d2bf5224246f892be&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.9.343.4&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.203</a> state that the following may be added in the production of standard honey wine:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Water to facilitate fermentation, provided the density of the honey and water mixture is not reduced below 13 degrees Brix;</li> 
             <li>Hops in quantities not to exceed one pound for each 1,000 pounds of honey; and</li> 
             <li>Pure, dry sugar or honey for sweetening. Sugar may be added only after fermentation is completed.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>After complete fermentation or complete fermentation and sweetening, the wine may not have an alcohol content of more than 14 percent by volume or a total solids content that exceeds 35 degrees Brix.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu18" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw18" name="hw18"></a>HW18: Do I need to obtain TTB formula approval before producing standard honey wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub18" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>All domestically produced agricultural wines, including honey wine, are subject to the formula requirements in <a href="/wine/laws-regulations-and-public-guidance">27 CFR 24.201</a>. However, in <a href="/images/pdfs/rulings/2016-2.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Ruling 2016-2</a> TTB approved general-use formulas for certain standard agricultural wines made from honey, dried fruit, or certain other agricultural products listed in the ruling and produced in accordance with the standards set forth in <a href="/wine/laws-regulations-and-public-guidance">27 CFR 24, subpart I</a>. As a result, a person producing honey wine in accordance with 27 CFR  24.200 and 24.203 is granted approval of a general-use formula and does not need to submit a formula to TTB. As provided in  24.200, standard agricultural honey wine may not be colored or flavored (except that hops may be used as outlined in  24.203), may not contain added spirits, and may not consist of wine made from one agricultural product that is blended with wine made from another agricultural product. Section 24.203 contains limitations on the use of sugar and water in the production of standard honey wine. Producers of honey wines that are not made in accordance with these regulatory standards must still submit a formula to TTB. Please see <a href="/images/pdfs/rulings/2016-2.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Ruling 2016-2</a> for additional information.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 12/19/2017</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu19" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw19" name="hw19"></a>HW19: Does the IRC allow me to produce honey wine that is not a standard agricultural wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub19" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you wish to add coloring or flavoring materials (other than hops) to a honey wine base, or if you wish to blend honey wine with other wine, you must first obtain approval of a formula for an "other than standard" (OTS) wine under the IRC. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=9912bb866bbb6880ef1377ecb2a31830&amp;node=se27.1.24_1218&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.218</a>.) (Also see <a href="#hw10">HW10</a> and <a href="#hw14">HW14</a> for information about the labeling of such products.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu20" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw20" name="hw20"></a>HW20: What IRC labeling requirements apply to all honey wine removed from bonded wine premises?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub20" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Although TTB's FAA Act wine labeling regulations in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr4_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a> do not apply to wine that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, IRC labeling requirements and TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=ee0120a170c6ea39134c9e711d9affc9&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.14&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.257</a> apply to all wines removed from wine premises. Under TTB regulations, labels must include the following information:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Name and address of the wine premises;</li> 
             <li>Brand name;</li> 
             <li>Alcohol content;</li> 
             <li>Net contents of the container; and</li> 
             <li>Kind of wine, which means  
              <ul> 
               <li>Class or type in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr4_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 4</a>; or</li> 
               <li>An adequate statement of composition for wines not subject to <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr4_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 4</a>. Statements of composition must include enough information to identify the tax class when viewed with the alcohol content.</li> 
              </ul> </li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Containers larger than 4 liters removed from the winery for consumption or sale must be labeled with this information as required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6e45cd360029c5f4828200781959393&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.12.343.16&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.259</a>. The required information may be cut, printed, or otherwise legibly and durably marked upon the container or placed on a label or tag securely affixed to the container.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu21" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw21" name="hw21"></a>HW21: Do I need to obtain a permit from TTB to produce honey wine (mead) for commercial purposes?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub21" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. If you are producing wine (including mead) that is at least 0.5 percent alcohol by volume for commercial purposes, you must first establish winery premises, obtain a bond, and receive permission from TTB to operate. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f2797979c9c7cb4918f0569f0d09437f&amp;node=sp27.1.24.d&amp;rgn=div6" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 24, subpart D</a>.) In addition, the FAA Act requires that anyone wishing to engage in the business of producing or blending wine (including mead) that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume must first obtain a basic permit from TTB. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr1_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 1</a>.) A basic permit under the FAA Act is not required for producers who only make wine that is less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. For more information on qualification requirements, see <a href="https://www.ttb.gov/wine/federal-application-process">https://www.ttb.gov/wine/federal-application-process</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu22" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw22" name="hw22"></a>HW22: What are the Federal excise tax rates for wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub22" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Wine is taxed at the excise tax rate appropriate to the wine product (with special rules for certain types of cider). (See <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2013-title26/pdf/USCODE-2013-title26-subtitleE-chap51-subchapA-partI-subpartC-sec5041.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">26 U.S.C. 5041</a>.) Products removed from a bonded wine premises that contain less than 0.5 percent alcohol by volume are not taxable as wine. The following are the basic tax classification categories for wine:</p> 
            <p>Still wine (containing not more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters):</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Not more than 14 percent alcohol by volume: $ 1.07 per wine gallon</li> 
             <li>Over 14 and not more than 21 percent alcohol by volume: $ 1.57 per wine gallon</li> 
             <li>Over 21 and not more than 24 percent alcohol by volume: $ 3.15 per wine gallon</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>Effervescent wine (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters):</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Artificially Carbonated Wine: $ 3.30 per wine gallon</li> 
             <li>Sparkling Wine: $ 3.40 per wine gallon</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>In addition, domestic wine producers may be eligible for a small producer tax credit based on their volume of production, as described in <a href="#hw24">HW 24</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu23" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw23" name="hw23"></a>HW23: For tax purposes, what is the difference between "sparkling" wine and "artificially carbonated" wine?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub23" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>A "sparkling wine" is made effervescent (containing more than 0.392 grams of carbon dioxide per 100 milliliters of wine) by carbon dioxide resulting solely from the secondary fermentation of the wine within a closed container, tank or bottle. A wine made effervescent in any other way (such as injection of carbon dioxide) is considered artificially carbonated. (See <a href="#hw11">HW11</a> and <a href="#hw12">HW12</a> for information about the labeling of these products.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu24" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw24" name="hw24"></a>HW24: Are mead producers eligible for the small producer tax credit?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub24" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Like other domestic wine producers, domestic mead producers are eligible for the small producer tax credit if they produce not more than 250,000 gallons of wine during a calendar year. The credit may be taken on the first 100,000 gallons of wine removed for consumption or sale by an eligible person during a calendar year, and it does not apply to sparkling wine (including sparkling mead) or to wine that is not produced at a qualified bonded wine premises in the United States. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f5749a5eb03dd06791b6529e60f3841e&amp;node=se27.1.24_1278&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.278</a> for more details about the conditions for taking the credit.) For additional information on computing the tax credit, please see TTB's <a href="/tax-audit/quick-reference-guide-to-wine-excise-tax" target="_blank">Quick Reference Guide to Wine Excise Tax</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu25" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw25" name="hw25"></a>HW25: I import honey wine. What TTB requirements apply to my operations?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub25" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If you import honey wine (mead) that contains not less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, you must:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li><a href="/applications">Obtain an Importer's Basic Permit</a> under the Federal Alcohol Administration Act;</li> 
             <li>Submit a pre-import letter (or formula) in accordance with Industry Circular 2007-4;</li> 
             <li>Obtain a <a href="/images/pdfs/forms/f510031.pdf" target="_blank">certificate of label approval</a>;</li> 
             <li>Label the wine in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=f6bd51e4d0db630348530e98808de566&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.2&amp;rgn=div5" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 4</a>;</li> 
             <li>Label the wine with the health warning statement required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>;</li> 
             <li>Pay the appropriate wine excise tax rate for the wine (<a href="#hw22">see HW22</a>).</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>If you also import wine (including mead) that is at least 0.5 percent alcohol by volume and less than 7 percent alcohol by volume, the following requirements apply:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Label the wine in accordance with all applicable FDA food labeling requirements;</li> 
             <li>Label the wine with the Health Warning Statement required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=e6c94daa4b9e02f0bcb844088a61d75a&amp;rgn=div5&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.12&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 16</a>;</li> 
             <li>Pay the appropriate excise tax rate (<a href="#hw22">see HW 22</a>);</li> 
             <li>Label the wine in accordance with <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr27_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR part 27</a>.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>You do <em>not</em> need to obtain an Importer's Basic Permit if you import <em>only</em> wine that contains less than 7 percent alcohol by volume. Additional information regarding the importation of alcohol beverages into the U.S. may be found on the <a href="/itd/importing-bottled-alcohol-beverages-into-the-united-states">International Affairs Division webpage</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu26" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw26" name="hw26"></a>HW26: May a brewer produce honey wine (mead) on brewery premises?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub26" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>No. TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=01e75612633a3a67a1d5db9e625e804c&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.20.3.371.3&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 25.23</a> do not permit the production of wine (including mead) on brewery premises. However, brewers may apply to TTB for permission to alternate the use of a portion of the brewery with a winery premises as outlined in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=01e75612633a3a67a1d5db9e625e804c&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.20.7.377.17&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 25.81</a>.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu27" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw27" name="hw27"></a>HW27: I would like to blend honey wine (mead) and beer. Is that permitted under TTB regulations?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub27" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>No. Under Federal law and TTB regulations, the blending of wine (including mead) and beer is not an authorized operation at a winery (see <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c0c8972ca0bafd6c2368bbd27d928a9e&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.19.4.337.2&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.101</a>) or a brewery (see <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?c=ecfr&amp;SID=c0c8972ca0bafd6c2368bbd27d928a9e&amp;rgn=div8&amp;view=text&amp;node=27:1.0.1.1.20.3.371.3&amp;idno=27" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 25.23</a>). Under TTB regulations, beer must be brewed from malt or from a substitute for malt. However, a brewer may use honey as an adjunct in the fermentation of beer on brewery premises, subject to the regulations in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=c6cc5abf6cca179c73093e2e7d18db11&amp;tpl=/ecfrbrowse/Title27/27cfr25_main_02.tpl" target="_blank" rel="noopener">part 25</a>. (See <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=ea1a201a08902a09a2102cfc92809450&amp;node=se27.1.25_115&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 25.15(a)</a>.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 02/17/2015</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="HWmenu28" class="mmenu">
           <a id="hw28" name="hw28"></a>HW28: May I label a malt beverage fermented from honey and malted barley as "mead"?
          </div> 
          <div id="HWsub28" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>No. Because "mead" is viewed as a synonym for "honey wine," it is TTB's position that the term may not be used as a designation for a malt beverage. The term "braggot" may be used as additional information on the label of such a product, but it would not suffice as the designation by itself. (See <a href="/images/pdfs/rulings/ttb-ruling-2015-1-malt-beverage-formulas.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Ruling 2015-1</a> for more information about the appropriate designation of malt beverages made with honey.)</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 05/20/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
         <p>&nbsp;</p> 
         <div id="print_sch" class="print_sch">
          <a id="sch" name="sch"></a> 
          <p id="printIcon" class="printIcon" align="right"><a onclick="printContent('print_sch')" href="#sch"><img src="/images/printer.gif" alt="Print" width="24" height="24" align="top" border="0" hspace="4">Print</a></p> 
          <h3>Shelf Plans and Shelf Schematics</h3> 
          <p><a href="/images/pdfs/rulings/2016-1.pdf" target="_blank">TTB Ruling 2016-1</a>, The Shelf Plan and Shelf Schematic Exception to the "Tied House" Prohibition, and Activities Outside Such Exception</p> 
          <div id="SCHmenu1" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch1" name="sch1"></a>SSQ1: Why was TTB Ruling 2016-1 issued?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub1" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB issued Ruling 2016-1 in response to complaints from a number of industry members who expressed concerns over potential abuse of the narrow Tied-House exception provided for in 27 CFR 6.99(b), which removes <span style="text-decoration: underline;">only</span> the act of providing a recommended shelf plan or shelf schematic from the prohibited means to induce enumerated in 27 U.S.C. 205(b)(3).&nbsp; As a result of these complaints, TTB conducted a limited market review pertaining to traditional category management activities and the concerns that were brought to its attention.&nbsp; As a result of its market review, TTB found that there is a misunderstanding about what activities are permitted under the  6.99(b) exception.&nbsp; Accordingly, TTB issued TTB Ruling 2016-1 to clarify what is and is not exempted under such exception.&nbsp;</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu2" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch2" name="sch2"></a>SSQ2: Why didnt TTB issue a Notice of Proposed Rulemaking?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub2" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>It was not necessary.&nbsp; TTB Ruling 2016-1 merely provides guidance as to the plain meaning of the existing regulation under 27 CFR 6.99(b).&nbsp; It does not change TTBs longstanding position, nor does it change the meaning of the plain language of this regulatory exception.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu3" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch3" name="sch3"></a>SSQ3: Does TTB Ruling 2016-1 prohibit industry members from acting as category captains or from engaging in category management activities?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub3" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The terms category management and category captain are not defined in the TTB regulations and can mean different things to different industry members.&nbsp; TTB Ruling 2016-1 reminds the industry what is exempted by 27 CFR 6.99(b); specifically, providing a recommended shelf plan or shelf schematic, and nothing further.&nbsp; TTB considers additional services or things of value not specifically exempted by a subpart D exception, whether or not referenced as category management services, as inducements under 27 CFR 6.41 of the TTB regulations.&nbsp; Many of the common services given under the term category management/category captain may be considered things of value and, as such, inducements under the Tied-House statute and regulations.&nbsp; If such practice results in exclusion of competitor products, in whole or in part, such that the retailers independence is at risk, with the requisite connection to interstate or foreign commerce, and if (in the case of malt beverages only) there is a similar State law, the practice would violate the FAA Act.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu4" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch4" name="sch4"></a>SSQ4: Are there certain services or things of value that are not considered inducements under the Tied-House regulations?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub4" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Subject to specific recordkeeping requirements, the subpart D exceptions found in 27 CFR part 6 that allow industry members to provide a recommended shelf plan also generally authorize providing certain other things of value, such as:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Product displays not to exceed $300 per brand.</li> 
             <li>Point of sale/consumer advertising items such as posters, coasters, paper napkins, foam scrapers, calendars, ash trays, cork screws, shirts and caps.</li> 
             <li>Consumer coupons and direct contests.</li> 
             <li>Consumer tastings and samplings.</li> 
            </ul> Providing a service or thing of value to a retailer that is not specifically exempted under the 27 CFR part 6 subpart D exceptions, constitutes an inducement under the Tied-House provisions. 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu5" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch5" name="sch5"></a>SSQ5: What does TTB mean when it says in Ruling 2016-1 "This Ruling illustrates what a shelf plan or shelf schematic is, and is not, relative to the exception in 27 CFR 6.99(b). A shelf plan or shelf schematic provided under this exception is a simple sales tool offering options as to how an industry member thinks a retailers shelves should appear.
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub5" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The TTB regulations at 27 CFR 6.99(b) state that the act by an industry member of providing a recommended shelf plan or shelf schematic for distilled spirits, wine, or malt beverages does not constitute a means to induce within the meaning of section 105(b)(3) of the Act.</p> In any potential investigation, TTB will look in part to determine if shelf plans or schematics are being provided as mere recommendations (authorized under the subpart D exceptions) or the de facto ultimate plan adopted by the retailer as a result of a unique relationship between it and the submitting industry member.&nbsp; In the latter scenario, TTB may argue the submitting industry members schematic does not qualify for the section 6.99(b) exception. 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu6" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch6" name="sch6"></a>SSQ6: In TTB Ruling 2016-1, what does TTB mean by [h]owever, as revealed by TTBs review of industry practices, some industry members do not offer a mere shelving plan alone but also include additional services, sometimes of significant value, that exceed the 27 CFR 6.99(b) exceptions plain language.
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub6" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>During its review of industry practices, TTB found services being provided to retailers that are not specifically exempted under part 6, subpart D of the TTB regulations.&nbsp; (27 CFR part 6, subpart D).&nbsp; Industry members should read the Tied-House regulations to determine if the activities, services or items of value they furnish retailers are <strong>specifically exempted</strong> under subpart D.&nbsp; If not <strong>specifically exempted</strong>, then such activities, services or items of value constitute inducements.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu7" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch7" name="sch7"></a>SSQ7: Are any of the five examples listed in TTB Ruling 2016-1 red light activities or are they all yellow light activities?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub7" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The terms red light and yellow light are neither used nor defined in the FAA Act or TTBs Tied-House regulations.</p> 
            <p>The five examples of additional services (which are not all inclusive) described in TTB Ruling 2016-1 are not exempted by 27 CFR 6.99(b). Such services therefore constitute inducements within the meaning of section 105(b)(3) of the FAA Act.</p> 
            <p>In order to determine if the retailers independence is at risk, TTB would first determine if an inducement has been given to the retailer. It would then evaluate, in their entirety, all of the industry members practices/activities with the retailer and determine if they meet the requirements outlined in 27 CFR 6.152 (Practices which put retailer independence at risk) and/or 6.153 (Criteria for determining retailer independence).&nbsp; TTB makes these determinations on a case-by-case basis.</p> A Federal Tied-House violation will exist if all of the elements of a violation are present.&nbsp; This includes an industry member providing a retailer with a prohibited inducement that results in exclusion of competitor products, in whole or in part, such that the retailers independence is at risk, with the requisite connection to interstate or foreign commerce, and (in the case of malt beverages only) a similar State law. 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu8" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch8" name="sch8"></a>SSQ8: In TTB Ruling 2016-1, how would TTB determine if an industry member is [a]ssuming, in whole or in part, a retailers purchasing or pricing decisions, or shelf stocking decisions involving a competitors products?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub8" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>TTB will examine industry and retailer relationships on a case-by-case basis to determine whether the activity constitutes a proscribed inducement under 27 U.S.C. 205(b)(1)  (b)(7), as well as whether the activity places the retailers independence at risk according to the criteria listed in 27 CFR 6.152 and 6.153.&nbsp; Whether an industry members activities place a retailers independence at risk is especially relevant when only one industry member prepares and submits schematics for a retailer.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu9" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch9" name="sch9"></a>SSQ9: Can an industry member receive and analyze confidential and/or proprietary competitor information on behalf of the retailer?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub9" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>No subpart D exception authorizes analysis of retailer or competitor information (on behalf of the retailer) that is confidential/proprietary and not made available to all industry members on the same terms.&nbsp; Accordingly, TTB considers such services a thing of value, and therefore an inducement, whether or not they ultimately result in furnishing a recommended schematic to the retailer.</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Example  Under a retailer-initiated program, competing industry members sales data, planned promotions, new products, or product pricing are shared with one industry member, which uses that information (in whole or in part) to create and provide the retailer with a recommended shelf plan. <br><br></li> 
             <li>Example  Under an industry member-initiated program, retailer information (such as confidential retailer sales goals and expectations or retailer proprietary sales data) is shared with only one industry member, which uses such data (in whole or in part) to create and provide the retailer with a recommended shelf plan.</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu10" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch10" name="sch10"></a>SSQ10: Can an industry member furnish retailers with items of value, including market data from third party vendors?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub10" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>While TTB realizes industry members often purchase and use third-party market data to assist their creation of a recommended schematic, there is no subpart D exception that authorizes sharing with or giving to retailers such information in any format other than the ultimate shelf schematic recommended by the industry member.&nbsp; It is up to the retailer (and not the industry member) to obtain and analyze any data that potentially supports or refutes the utility of a recommended schematic it receives.</p> Moreover, because there is also no subpart D exception authorizing industry members to purchase third-party market data from a company that is owned or controlled by the retailer, industry members doing so risk being deemed to have indirectly (and unlawfully) induced the retailers future purchases to the exclusion of its competitors. 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu11" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch11" name="sch11"></a>SSQ11: Can an industry member provide follow-up services (such as communicating with the retailers stores, vendors, representatives, wholesalers, and suppliers concerning daily operational matters such as store resets, add and delete item lists, advertisements and promotions) in order to monitor and revise a schematic?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub11" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>There is no subpart D exception authorizing industry members to monitor (or assist in monitoring) the retailers compliance with a shelf plan on behalf of a retailer.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHmenu12" class="mmenu">
           <a id="sch12" name="sch12"></a>SSQ12: Can an industry member furnish a retailer with human resources to perform functions/activities other than stocking, rotation or pricing the industry members own product, as permitted in  6.99(a) of the TTB regulations?
          </div> 
          <div id="SCHsub12" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>If an industry member furnishes human resources to perform any function on behalf of the retailer that is not specifically exempted by the Tied-House regulations, such services constitute an inducement.&nbsp; Industry members should carefully read the Tied-House regulations and determine if any contemplated activities, services or items of value are specifically exempted under subpart D.&nbsp; If not specifically exempted then the activities, services or items of value constitute inducements.&nbsp; If such practice results in exclusion of competitor products, in whole or in part, such that the retailers independence is at risk, with the requisite connection to interstate or foreign commerce, and if (in the case of malt beverages only) there is a similar State law, the practice would violate the FAA Act.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 03/15/2016</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
         <p>&nbsp;</p> 
         <div id="print_nd" class="print_nd">
          <a id="ND" name="ND"></a> 
          <p id="printIcon" class="printIcon" align="right"><a onclick="printContent('print_nd')" href="#nd"><img src="/images/printer.gif" alt="Print" width="24" height="24" align="top" border="0" hspace="4">Print</a></p> 
          <h3><strong>Natural Disaster Frequently Asked Questions</strong></h3> 
          <p>&nbsp;</p> 
          <p><strong>Wine</strong></p> 
          <div id="NDmenu1" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ND1" name="ND1"></a>NDQ1: I lost a significant quantity of wine at my bonded premises as a result of a natural disaster. Am I required to report my losses to TTB?
          </div> 
          <div id="NDsub1" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>Yes. TTB regulations at <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=0453724ba4495691a00478ce678a2365&amp;node=se27.1.24_1268&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.268 </a>require that proprietors report any casualty loss immediately to TTB. However, depending on the circumstances of the natural disaster, TTB may extend the deadline on a case-by-case basis.&nbsp;&nbsp; Proprietors should report their losses as soon as they are able to determine the extent of the damage to their inventories. In their submissions, proprietors should provide the following information:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Name and address of the bonded wine premises</li> 
             <li>Registry number of the bonded wine premises ("BWN/BWC/BW-State-xxxxx")</li> 
             <li>Kind of wine</li> 
             <li>Alcohol content</li> 
             <li>Approximate volume in gallons</li> 
             <li>Date of loss</li> 
             <li>Reason for loss (i.e., fire damage)</li> 
             <li>Printed name of winery representative</li> 
             <li>Signature (Person signing must have signing authority)</li> 
             <li>Telephone number</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>&nbsp;</p> 
            <p>This information should be submitted, either in letter form or as a fax, to TTB's National Revenue Center:</p> 
            <p>550 Main Street, Suite 8002<br> Cincinnati, OH 45202-5215<br> Toll-free: 877-882-3277<br> Fax: 202-453-2338</p> The volume of wine lost must also be reported on <a href="/wine/report-of-wine-premises-operations">TTB F 5120.17, Report of Wine Premises Operations</a>, for the reporting period during which the loss occurred. 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 10/23/2017</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="NDmenu2" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ND2" name="ND2"></a>NDQ2: What records should I keep of any spillage and breakage that occurred on my winery premises as a result of a natural disaster?
          </div> 
          <div id="NDsub2" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>You should document the volume and tax class of wine lost in all appropriate TTB-required records - for example, the bulk still wine record (see <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=fdad319d893f703ce4668b1b62725eb9&amp;node=se27.1.24_1301&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.301</a>) and the bottled or packed wine record (see <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=fdad319d893f703ce4668b1b62725eb9&amp;node=se27.1.24_1308&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.308</a>). Additionally, you must report the loss on your next <a href="/wine/report-of-wine-premises-operations" target="_blank" rel="noopener">TTB F 5120.17, Report of Wine Premises Operations</a>. The volume of bulk wine lost should be reported on line 29 of Part 1, Section A; the volume of bottled wine lost should be reported on line 18 of Part 1, Section B.</p> 
            <p><span class="crumbs">Last reviewed/updated 10/23/2017</span></p> 
            <p><a href="#content">Return to Top</a></p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <div id="NDmenu3" class="mmenu">
           <a id="ND3" name="ND3"></a>NDQ3: I need to destroy untaxpaid wine and/or wine spirits that were damaged during a natural disaster. What TTB procedures do I need to follow?
          </div> 
          <div id="NDsub3" class="subMenu"> 
           <div class="subs"> 
            <p>The destruction of wine is provided for in <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=8ec407ef38bcba4a505fac97cd525d74&amp;node=se27.1.24_1294&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.294</a>, which requires that a proprietor first request permission from TTB's National Revenue Center to destroy the lot of wine before proceeding with the destruction. &nbsp;Depending on the circumstances of the natural disaster, TTB may waive the requirement that proprietors must first obtain TTB approval before destroying the wine.&nbsp; However, proprietors must still submit a notification, as soon as practical, to the NRC containing the information required by <a href="http://www.ecfr.gov/cgi-bin/text-idx?SID=8ec407ef38bcba4a505fac97cd525d74&amp;node=se27.1.24_1294&amp;rgn=div8" target="_blank" rel="noopener">27 CFR 24.294</a>. This notification should include the following information:</p> 
            <ul> 
             <li>Date of the letter</li> 
             <li>Name and address of the bonded wine premises</li> 
             <li>Registry number of the bonded wine premises ("BWN/BWC/BW-State-xxxxx")</li> 
             <li>Kind of wine</li> 
             <li>Alcohol content</li> 
             <li>Approximate volume in gallons</li> 
             <li>Where wine was destroyed</li> 
             <li>Date of destruction</li> 
             <li>Reason for destruction</li> 
             <li>Printed name of winery representative</li> 
             <li>Signature (Person signing must have signing authority)</li> 
             <li>Telephone number</li> 
            </ul> 
            <p>The notification should be faxed to the National Revenue Center a
